</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Grammatical_categories" title="Template:Grammatical categories"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Grammatical_categories" title="Template talk:Grammatical categories"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Grammatical_categories" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Grammatical categories"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A <b>grammatical case</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a> and noun modifiers (<a href="/wiki/Determiner" title="Determiner">determiners</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjectives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Participle" title="Participle">participles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Numeral_(linguistics)" title="Numeral (linguistics)">numerals</a>) that corresponds to one or more potential <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_function" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammatical function">grammatical functions</a> for a <a href="/wiki/Nominal_group_(functional_grammar)" title="Nominal group (functional grammar)">nominal group</a> in a wording.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In various languages, nominal groups consisting of a noun and its modifiers belong to one of a few such categories. For instance, in <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, one says <i>I see them</i> and <i>they see me</i>: the <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a> pronouns <i>I/they</i> represent the perceiver and the <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a> pronouns <i>me/them</i> represent the phenomenon perceived. Here, nominative and accusative are cases, that is, categories of pronouns corresponding to the functions they have in representation.
</p><p>English has largely lost its inflected case system but personal pronouns still have three cases, which are simplified forms of the nominative, accusative (including functions formerly handled by the <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> cases. They are used with <a href="/wiki/Personal_pronoun" title="Personal pronoun">personal pronouns</a>: <a href="/wiki/Subjective_case" class="mw-redirect" title="Subjective case">subjective case</a> (I, you, he, she, it, we, they, who, whoever), <a href="/wiki/Objective_case" class="mw-redirect" title="Objective case">objective case</a> (me, you, him, her, it, us, them, whom, whomever) and <a href="/wiki/Possessive_case" class="mw-redirect" title="Possessive case">possessive case</a> (my, mine; your, yours; his; her, hers; its; our, ours; their, theirs; whose; whosever).<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Forms such as <i>I</i>, <i>he</i> and <i>we</i> are used for the <a href="/wiki/Subject_(grammar)" title="Subject (grammar)">subject</a> ("<b>I</b> kicked John"), and forms such as <i>me</i>, <i>him</i> and <i>us</i> are used for the <a href="/wiki/Object_(grammar)" title="Object (grammar)">object</a> ("John kicked <b>me</b>").
</p><p>As a language evolves, cases can merge (for instance, in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">locative case</a> merged with the dative), a phenomenon known as <a href="/wiki/Syncretism_(linguistics)" title="Syncretism (linguistics)">syncretism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClackson200791_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClackson200791-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Languages such as <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kannada" title="Kannada">Kannada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> have extensive case systems, with nouns, pronouns, adjectives, and determiners all inflecting (usually by means of different <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffixes</a>) to indicate their case. The number of cases differs between languages: <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> has three; modern English has three but for pronouns only; <a href="/wiki/Torlakian_dialects" title="Torlakian dialects">Torlakian dialects</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a> have three; <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demotic_Greek" title="Demotic Greek">Modern Greek</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Irish_Language" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Language">Irish</a> have four; <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a> and Ancient Greek have five; <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>, Latin, Russian, <a href="/wiki/Slovak_language" title="Slovak language">Slovak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kajkavian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Kajkavian language">Kajkavian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovenian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Slovenian language">Slovenian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> each have at least six; <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgian_language" title="Georgian language">Georgian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbo-Croatian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Serbo-Croatian language">Serbo-Croatian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a> have seven; <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marathi_language" title="Marathi language">Marathi</a>, Sanskrit, Kannada, Tamil, <a href="/wiki/Telugu_language" title="Telugu language">Telugu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malayalam" title="Malayalam">Malayalam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assamese_language" title="Assamese language">Assamese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenlandic_language" title="Greenlandic language">Greenlandic</a> have eight; <a href="/wiki/Old_Nubian" title="Old Nubian">Old Nubian</a> had nine; <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque</a> has 13; <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a> has 14; <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a> has 15; <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a> has 18; and <a href="/wiki/Tsez_language" title="Tsez language">Tsez</a> has at least 36 cases.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Commonly encountered cases include <a href="/wiki/Nominative" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominative">nominative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Accusative" class="mw-redirect" title="Accusative">accusative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dative" class="mw-redirect" title="Dative">dative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genitive" class="mw-redirect" title="Genitive">genitive</a>. A role that one of those languages marks by case is often marked in English with a <a href="/wiki/Preposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Preposition">preposition</a>. For example, the English prepositional phrase <i>with (his) foot</i> (as in "John kicked the ball with his foot") might be rendered in Russian using a single noun in the <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_case" title="Instrumental case">instrumental case</a>, or in Ancient Greek as <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">τῷ ποδί</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek transliteration" lang="grc-Latn"><i>tôi podí</i></span>, meaning "the foot") with both words (the definite article, and the noun <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">πούς</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek transliteration" lang="grc-Latn"><i>poús</i></span>) "foot") changing to dative form.
</p><p>More formally, case has been defined as "a system of marking dependent nouns for the type of relationship they bear to their <a href="/wiki/Head_(linguistics)" title="Head (linguistics)">heads</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Blake_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blake-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p.1">&#58;&#8202;p.1&#8202;</span></sup> Cases should be distinguished from <a href="/wiki/Thematic_relation" title="Thematic relation">thematic roles</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Agent_(grammar)" title="Agent (grammar)">agent</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Patient_(grammar)" title="Patient (grammar)">patient</a></i>. They are often closely related, and in languages such as Latin, several thematic roles are realised by a somewhat fixed case for deponent verbs, but cases are a <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">syntagmatic/phrasal</a> category, and thematic roles are the function of a syntagma/phrase in a larger structure. Languages having cases often exhibit <a href="/wiki/Free_word_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Free word order">free word order</a>, as thematic roles are not required to be marked by position in the sentence.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>It is widely accepted that the Ancient Greeks had a certain idea of the forms of a name in their own language. A fragment of <a href="/wiki/Anacreon" title="Anacreon">Anacreon</a> seems to prove this. Grammatical cases were first recognized by the <a href="/wiki/Stoics" class="mw-redirect" title="Stoics">Stoics</a> and from some philosophers of the <a href="/wiki/Peripatetic_school" title="Peripatetic school">Peripatetic school</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-treccani_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treccani-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The advancements of those philosophers were later employed by the philologists of the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">Library of Alexandria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-treccani_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treccani-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The English word <i>case</i> used in this sense comes from the Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">casus</i></span>, which is derived from the verb <i lang="la"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cado#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:cado">cadere</a></i>, "to fall", from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_root" title="Proto-Indo-European root">Proto-Indo-European root</a> <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Indo-European/%E1%B8%B1ad-" class="extiw" title="wikt:Appendix:Proto-Indo-European/ḱad-"><span title="Proto-Indo-European language" class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;"><span lang="ine">*ḱad-</span></span></a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Latin word is a <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a> of the Greek <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">πτῶσις</span></span>, <span title="Ancient Greek transliteration" lang="grc-Latn"><i>ptôsis</i></span>, lit. "falling, fall".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The sense is that all other cases are considered to have "fallen" away from the nominative. This imagery is also reflected in the word <i><a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">declension</a></i>, from Latin <i lang="la"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/declino#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:declino">declinere</a></i>, "to lean", from the PIE root <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Indo-European/%E1%B8%B1ley-" class="extiw" title="wikt:Appendix:Proto-Indo-European/ḱley-"><span title="Proto-Indo-European language" class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;"><span lang="ine">*ḱley-</span></span></a></i>.
</p><p>The equivalent to "case" in several other European languages also derives from <i>casus</i>, including <i lang="fr"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cas#French" class="extiw" title="wikt:cas">cas</a></i> in French, <i lang="it"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/caso#Italian" class="extiw" title="wikt:caso">caso</a></i> in Italian and <i lang="de"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Kasus#German" class="extiw" title="wikt:Kasus">Kasus</a></i> in German. The Russian word <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">паде́ж</span></span> (<i>padyézh</i>) is a calque from Greek and similarly contains a root meaning "fall", and the German <i lang="de"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Fall#German" class="extiw" title="wikt:Fall">Fall</a></i> and Czech <i lang="cs"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/p%C3%A1d#Czech" class="extiw" title="wikt:pád">pád</a></i> simply mean "fall", and are used for both the concept of grammatical case and to refer to physical falls. The Dutch equivalent <i lang="nl"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/naamval#Dutch" class="extiw" title="wikt:naamval">naamval</a></i> translates as 'noun case', in which 'noun' has the older meaning of both 'adjective (noun)' and '(substantive) noun'. The Finnish equivalent is <i lang="fi"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sija#Finnish" class="extiw" title="wikt:sija">sija</a></i>, whose main meaning is "position" or "place".
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Indo-European_languages">Indo-European languages</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Indo-European languages"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:E7127-Balakhna-500-Years.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/E7127-Balakhna-500-Years.jpg/220px-E7127-Balakhna-500-Years.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/E7127-Balakhna-500-Years.jpg/330px-E7127-Balakhna-500-Years.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/E7127-Balakhna-500-Years.jpg/440px-E7127-Balakhna-500-Years.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a><figcaption>On this sign in Russian memorializing an anniversary of the city of <a href="/wiki/Balakhna" title="Balakhna">Balakhna</a>, the word <i>Balakhna (</i><a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru">Балахн<b>а</b></span><i>)</i> on the right is in the nominative case, whereas the word <i>Balakhne (</i><a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru">Балахн<b>е</b></span><i>)</i> is in the dative case in <i>Balakhne 500 Let</i> ('Balakhna is 500 years old', literally '[There is] 500 years to Balakhna') on the front of the sign. Furthermore, <i>let</i> is in the genitive (plural) case.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Although not very prominent in modern English, cases featured much more saliently in <a href="/wiki/Old_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old English language">Old English</a> and other ancient <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>. Historically, the Indo-European languages had eight <b>morphological cases</b>, although modern languages typically have fewer, using prepositions and word order to convey information that had previously been conveyed using distinct noun forms. Among modern languages, cases still feature prominently in most of the <a href="/wiki/Balto-Slavic_languages" title="Balto-Slavic languages">Balto-Slavic languages</a> (except Macedonian and Bulgarian<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>), with most having six to eight cases, as well as <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Modern Greek</a>, which have four. In German, cases are mostly marked on articles and adjectives, and less so on nouns. In Icelandic, articles, adjectives, personal names and nouns are all marked for case, making it the most conservative <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic language</a>.
</p><p>The eight historical Indo-European cases are as follows, with examples either of the English case or of the English syntactic alternative to case:
</p>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>

</td>

<p>by hand
</p><p>with John
</p>
</td>
<td><i>We wiped the floor <b>with a mop</b>.</i>

</td>
<td>Corresponds to English prepositions <i>by</i>, <i>with</i> and <i>via</i> as well as synonymous constructions such as <i>using</i>, <i>by use of</i> and <i>through</i>.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>All of the above are just rough descriptions; the precise distinctions vary significantly from language to language, and as such they are often more complex. Case is based fundamentally on changes to the noun to indicate the noun's role in the sentence – one of the defining features of so-called <a href="/wiki/Fusional_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Fusional languages">fusional languages</a>. <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> was a fusional language, but Modern English does not work this way.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Modern_English">Modern English</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern English"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Modern <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> has largely abandoned the inflectional case system of <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> in favor of <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">analytic</a> constructions. The <a href="/wiki/Personal_pronoun" title="Personal pronoun">personal pronouns</a> of Modern English retain morphological case more strongly than any other word class (a remnant of the more extensive <a href="/wiki/Old_English_declension" class="mw-redirect" title="Old English declension">case system of Old English</a>). For other pronouns, and all nouns, adjectives, and articles, grammatical function is indicated only by <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Preposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Preposition">prepositions</a>, and by the "<a href="/wiki/Saxon_genitive" class="mw-redirect" title="Saxon genitive">Saxon genitive</a>" (<i>-'s</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>a<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Taken as a whole, <a href="/wiki/English_personal_pronouns" title="English personal pronouns">English personal pronouns</a> are typically said to have three morphological cases:
</p>
<ul><li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative case</a></i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Subjective_pronouns" class="mw-redirect" title="Subjective pronouns">subjective pronouns</a></i> such as <i>I</i>, <i>he</i>, <i>she</i>, <i>we</i>), used for the subject of a <a href="/wiki/Finite_verb" title="Finite verb">finite verb</a> and sometimes for the <a href="/wiki/Complement_(linguistics)" title="Complement (linguistics)">complement</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">copula</a>.</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Oblique_case" title="Oblique case">oblique case</a></i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Object_pronoun" title="Object pronoun">object pronouns</a></i> such as <i>me</i>, <i>him</i>, <i>her</i>, <i>us</i>), used for the direct or indirect <a href="/wiki/Object_(grammar)" title="Object (grammar)">object</a> of a verb, for the object of a preposition, for an absolute disjunct, and sometimes for the complement of a copula.</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive case</a></i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Possessive_pronouns" class="mw-redirect" title="Possessive pronouns">possessive pronouns</a></i> such as <i>my/mine</i>, <i>his</i>, <i>her/hers</i>, <i>our/ours</i>), used for a grammatical possessor. This is not always considered to be a case; see <a href="/wiki/English_possessive#Status_of_the_possessive_as_a_grammatical_case" title="English possessive">English possessive §&#160;Status of the possessive as a grammatical case</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Most English personal pronouns have five forms: the nominative case form, the oblique case form, a distinct <i><a href="/wiki/Reflexive_pronoun" title="Reflexive pronoun">reflexive</a></i> or <i>intensive</i> form (such as <i>myself</i>, <i>ourselves</i>) which is based upon the possessive determiner form but is coreferential to a preceding instance of nominative or oblique, and the possessive case forms, which include both a <i><a href="/wiki/Determiner_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Determiner (linguistics)">determiner</a></i> form (such as <i>my</i>, <i>our</i>) and a predicatively-used <i>independent</i> form (such as <i>mine</i>, <i>ours</i>) which is distinct (with two exceptions: the third person singular masculine <i>he</i> and the third person singular neuter <i>it</i>, which use the same form for both determiner and independent [<i>his car</i>, <i>it is his</i>]). The interrogative personal pronoun <i>who</i> exhibits the greatest diversity of forms within the modern English pronoun system, having definite nominative, oblique, and genitive forms (<i>who</i>, <i>whom</i>, <i>whose</i>) and equivalently-coordinating indefinite forms (<i>whoever</i>, <i>whomever</i>, and <i>whosever</i>).
</p><p>Although English <i>pronouns</i> can have subject and object forms (he/him, she/her), <i>nouns</i> show only a singular/plural and a possessive/non-possessive distinction (e.g. <i>chair</i>, <i>chairs</i>, <i>chair's</i>, <i>chairs'</i>); there is no manifest difference in the form of <i>chair</i> between "The chair is here." (subject) and "I own the chair." (direct object), a distinction made instead by word order and context.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Hierarchy_of_cases">Hierarchy of cases</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hierarchy of cases"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Case_hierarchy" title="Case hierarchy">Case hierarchy</a></div>
<p>Cases can be ranked in the following hierarchy, where a language that does not have a given case will tend not to have any cases to the right of the missing case:<sup id="cite_ref-Blake_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blake-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p.89">&#58;&#8202;p.89&#8202;</span></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a> <i>or</i>  <a href="/wiki/Absolutive_case" title="Absolutive case">absolutive</a> → <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a> <i>or</i> <a href="/wiki/Ergative_case" title="Ergative case">ergative</a> → <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> → <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a> → <a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">locative</a> <i>or</i> <a href="/wiki/Prepositional_case" class="mw-redirect" title="Prepositional case">prepositional</a> → <a href="/wiki/Ablative_case" title="Ablative case">ablative</a> <i>and/or</i> <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_case" title="Instrumental case">instrumental</a> → <i>others</i>.</dd></dl>
<p>This is, however, only a general tendency. Many forms of <a href="/wiki/Central_German" title="Central German">Central German</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Colognian_dialect" title="Colognian dialect">Colognian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luxembourgish" title="Luxembourgish">Luxembourgish</a>, have a dative case but lack a genitive. In <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> nouns, the nominative and accusative have fallen together, whereas the dative–locative has remained separate in some paradigms; Irish also has genitive and vocative cases. In many modern Indo-Aryan languages, the accusative, genitive, and dative have merged to an oblique case, but many of these languages still retain vocative, locative, and ablative cases. Old English had an instrumental case, but neither a locative nor a prepositional case.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Case_order">Case order</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Case order"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The traditional case order (nom-gen-dat-acc) was expressed for the first time in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Art_of_Grammar" title="The Art of Grammar">The Art of Grammar</a></i> in the 2nd century BC:
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1157697682">.mw-parser-output .verse_translation .translated{padding-left:2em!important}@media only screen and (max-width:43.75em){.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small td{display:block;padding-left:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small .translated{padding-left:0.5em!important}}</style>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="verse_translation" style="margin-left:1em !important">

<tbody><tr style="vertical-align:top">
<td><div style="font-style:roman;text-align:left" lang="" class="poem">
<p><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/el:%CE%A4%CE%AD%CF%87%CE%BD%CE%B7_%CE%93%CF%81%CE%B1%CE%BC%CE%BC%CE%B1%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BA%CE%AE" class="extiw" title="s:el:Τέχνη Γραμματική">Πτώσεις ὀνομάτων εἰσὶ πέντε· ὀρθή, γενική, δοτική, αἰτιατική, κλητική.</a>
</p>
</div>
</td>
<td class="translated"><div style="font-style:roman;text-align:left" lang="" class="poem">
<p>There are five Cases, the right [nominative], the generic [genitive], the dative, the accusative, and the vocative.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> grammars, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Ars_grammatica" title="Ars grammatica">Ars grammatica</a></i>, followed the Greek tradition, but added the <a href="/wiki/Ablative_case" title="Ablative case">ablative case</a> of Latin. Later other European languages also followed that Graeco-Roman tradition.
</p><p>However, for some languages, such as Latin, due to case <a href="/wiki/Syncretism_(linguistics)" title="Syncretism (linguistics)">syncretism</a> the order may be changed for convenience, where the accusative or the vocative cases are placed after the nominative and before the genitive. For example:
</p>
<table>

<td>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Case_concord_systems">Case concord systems</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Case concord systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the most common<sup id="cite_ref-Blake_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blake-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> case concord system, only the head-word (the noun) in a phrase is marked for case. This system appears in many <a href="/wiki/Papuan_languages" title="Papuan languages">Papuan languages</a> as well as in <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quechua_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Quechua language">Quechua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_languages" title="Dravidian languages">Dravidian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan</a>, and other languages. In <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Amazonian_languages" title="Amazonian languages">Amazonian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australian_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Australian languages">Australian languages</a>, only the phrase-final word (not necessarily the noun) is marked for case. In many <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-European language">Indo-European</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic languages</a>, case is marked on the noun, the determiner, and usually the adjective. Other systems are less common. In some languages, there is <a href="/wiki/Suffixaufnahme" title="Suffixaufnahme">double-marking</a> of a word as both genitive (to indicate semantic role) and another case such as accusative (to establish concord with the head noun).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Declension_paradigms">Declension paradigms</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Declension paradigms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">Declension</a></div>
<p>Declension is the process or result of altering nouns to the correct grammatical cases. Languages with rich nominal inflection (using grammatical cases for many purposes) typically have a number of identifiable declension classes, or groups of nouns with a similar pattern of case inflection or declension. Sanskrit has six declension classes, whereas Latin is traditionally considered to have <a href="/wiki/Latin_declension" title="Latin declension">five</a>, and Ancient Greek <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_grammar#Declensions" title="Ancient Greek grammar">three</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Beetham_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beetham-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, Slovak has <a href="/wiki/Slovak_declension" title="Slovak declension">fifteen noun declension classes</a>, five for each gender (the number may vary depending on which paradigms are counted or omitted, this mainly concerns those that modify declension of foreign words; refer to article).
</p><p>In Indo-European languages, declension patterns may depend on a variety of factors, such as <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a>, phonological environment, and irregular historical factors. Pronouns sometimes have separate paradigms. In some languages, particularly <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic languages</a>, a case may contain different groups of endings depending on whether the word is a <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">noun</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjective</a>. A single case may contain many different endings, some of which may even be derived from different roots. For example, in Polish, the genitive case has <i>-a, -u, -ów, -i/-y, -e-</i> for nouns, and <i>-ego, -ej, -ich/-ych</i> for adjectives. To a lesser extent, a noun's <a href="/wiki/Masculine_personal" class="mw-redirect" title="Masculine personal">animacy or humanness</a> may add another layer of complexity. For example, in Russian:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1209690778">.mw-parser-output .interlinear .bold{font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .interlinear .smallcaps{text-transform:uppercase;font-size:smaller}</style><div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ru" style="margin: 0px;">Кот</p><p lang="ru-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">Kot-∅</p><p style="margin: 0px;">cat-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="nominative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">NOM</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="animate" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">AN</abbr>.</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ru" style="margin: 0px;">ловит</p><p lang="ru-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">lóvit</p><p style="margin: 0px;">catches</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ru" style="margin: 0px;">мышей</p><p lang="ru-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">myshéy.</p><p style="margin: 0px;">mice</p></div><p style="display: none;">Кот ловит мышей</p><p style="display: none;">Kot-∅ lóvit myshéy.</p><p style="display: none;">cat-NOM.AN. catches mice</p><p style="clear: left;">(The) cat catches mice.</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1209690778"><div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ru" style="margin: 0px;">Столб</p><p lang="ru-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">Stolb-∅</p><p style="margin: 0px;">pillar-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="nominative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">NOM</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="inanimate gender" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">INAN</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ru" style="margin: 0px;">держит</p><p lang="ru-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">dérzhit</p><p style="margin: 0px;">holds</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ru" style="margin: 0px;">крышу</p><p lang="ru-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">krýshu.</p><p style="margin: 0px;">roof</p></div><p style="display: none;">Столб держит крышу</p><p style="display: none;">Stolb-∅ dérzhit krýshu.</p><p style="display: none;">pillar-NOM.INAN holds roof</p><p style="clear: left;">(The) pillar holds a/the roof)</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<p>vs.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1209690778"><div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ru" style="margin: 0px;">Пётр</p><p lang="ru-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">Pyotr</p><p style="margin: 0px;">Peter</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ru" style="margin: 0px;">гладит</p><p lang="ru-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">gládit</p><p style="margin: 0px;">strokes</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ru" style="margin: 0px;">кота</p><p lang="ru-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">kot-á</p><p style="margin: 0px;">cat-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="accusative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">ACC</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="animate" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">AN</abbr></p></div><p style="display: none;">Пётр гладит кота</p><p style="display: none;">Pyotr gládit kot-á</p><p style="display: none;">Peter strokes cat-ACC.AN</p><p style="clear: left;">Peter strokes a/the cat</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<p>and
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1209690778"><div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ru" style="margin: 0px;">Пётр</p><p lang="ru-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">Pyotr</p><p style="margin: 0px;">Peter</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ru" style="margin: 0px;">ломает</p><p lang="ru-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">lomáyet</p><p style="margin: 0px;">breaks</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="ru" style="margin: 0px;">столб</p><p lang="ru-Latn" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">stolb-∅</p><p style="margin: 0px;">pillar-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="accusative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">ACC</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="inanimate gender" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">INAN</abbr></p></div><p style="display: none;">Пётр ломает столб</p><p style="display: none;">Pyotr lomáyet stolb-∅</p><p style="display: none;">Peter breaks pillar-ACC.INAN</p><p style="clear: left;">Peter breaks a/the pillar</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Examples">Examples</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Examples"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Australian_Aboriginal_languages">Australian Aboriginal languages</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Australian Aboriginal languages"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Australian_Aboriginal_languages" title="Australian Aboriginal languages">Australian languages</a> represent a diversity of case paradigms in terms of their alignment (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Nominative%E2%80%93accusative_alignment" title="Nominative–accusative alignment">nominative-accusative</a> vs. <a href="/wiki/Ergative%E2%80%93absolutive_alignment" title="Ergative–absolutive alignment">ergative-absolutive</a>) and the morpho-syntactic properties of case inflection including where/how many times across a noun phrase the case morphology will appear. For typical <a href="/wiki/R-expression" title="R-expression">r-expression</a> noun phrases, most Australian languages follow a basic ERG-ABS template with additional cases for peripheral arguments; however, many Australian languages, the function of case marking extends beyond the prototypical function of specifying the syntactic and semantic relation of an NP to a predicate.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dench and Evans (1988)<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> use a five-part system for categorizing the functional roles of case marking in Australian languages. They are enumerated below as they appear in Senge (2015):<sup id="cite_ref-:0_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><b>Relational</b>: a suffix which represents syntactic or semantic roles of a noun phrase in clauses.</li>
<li><b>Adnominal</b>: a suffix which relates a noun phrase to another within the one noun phrase.</li>
<li><b>Referential</b>: a suffix which attaches to a noun phrase in agreement with another noun phrase which represents one of the core arguments in the clause.</li>
<li><b>Subordinating</b>: a suffix which attaches to elements of a subordinate clause. Its functions are: (i) specifying temporal or logical (typically, causal and purposive) relationships between two clauses (Temporal-subordinator); (ii) indicating coreferential relationships between arguments in the two clauses (Concord-subordinator).</li>
<li><b>Derivational</b>: a suffix which attaches to a bare stem before other case suffixes and create a new lexical item.</li></ol>
<p>To illustrate this paradigm in action, take the case-system of <a href="/wiki/Wanyjirra_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Wanyjirra language">Wanyjirra</a> for whose description Senge invokes this system. Each of the case markers functions in the prototypical relational sense, but many extend into these additional functions:
</p>

<p><a href="/wiki/Wanyjirra_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Wanyjirra language">Wanyjirra</a> is an example of a language in which case marking occurs on all sub-constituents of the NP; see the following example in which the demonstrative, head, and quantifier of the noun phrase all receive ergative marking:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1209690778"><div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">yalu-nggu</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="distal" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">DIST</abbr>-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="ergative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">ERG</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">mawun-du</p><p style="margin: 0px;">man-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="ergative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">ERG</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">gujarra-lu</p><p style="margin: 0px;">two-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="ergative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">ERG</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ngu=wula</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="realis mood" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">REAL</abbr>=<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="third person" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">3</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="augmentative" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">AUG</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="subject" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">SBJ</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">yunbarn-ana</p><p style="margin: 0px;">sing-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="present tense" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PRES</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">junba</p><p style="margin: 0px;">corroboree.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="absolutive case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">ABS</abbr></p></div><p style="display: none;">yalu-nggu mawun-du gujarra-lu ngu=wula yunbarn-ana junba</p><p style="display: none;">DIST-ERG man-ERG two-ERG REAL=3.AUG.SBJ sing-PRES corroboree.ABS</p><p style="clear: left;">Those two men are singing corroboree.</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<p>However, this is by no means always the case or even the norm for Australian languages. For many, case-affixes are considered special-clitics (i.e. phrasal-affixes, see Anderson 2005<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>) because they have a singular fixed position within the phrase. For <a href="/wiki/Bardi_language" title="Bardi language">Bardi</a>, the case marker usually appears on the first phrasal constituent<sup id="cite_ref-:1_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while the opposite is the case for <a href="/wiki/Wangkatja_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Wangkatja language">Wangkatja</a> (i.e. the case marker is attracted to the rightmost edge of the phrase).<sup id="cite_ref-:2_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> See the following examples respectively:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1209690778"><div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div><b>Bardi</b><sup id="cite_ref-:1_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">Boordiji-nim</p><p style="margin: 0px;">fat-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="ergative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">ERG</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">niiwandi</p><p style="margin: 0px;">tall</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">aamba</p><p style="margin: 0px;">man</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">i-na-m-boo-na</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="third person" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">3</abbr>-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="transitive" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">TR</abbr>-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="past tense" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PST</abbr>-poke-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="remote past tense" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">REM</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="past tense" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PST</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">aril</p><p style="margin: 0px;">fish</p></div><p style="display: none;">Boordiji-nim niiwandi aamba i-na-m-boo-na aril</p><p style="display: none;">fat-ERG tall man 3-TR-PST-poke-REM.PST fish</p><p style="clear: left;">The tall fat man speared a fish.</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1209690778"><div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div><b>Wangkatja</b><sup id="cite_ref-:2_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">tjitji</p><p style="margin: 0px;">child</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">warta</p><p style="margin: 0px;">tree</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">purlkana-ngka</p><p style="margin: 0px;">big-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="locative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: lining-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">LOC</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">nyinarra-nyi</p><p style="margin: 0px;">sitting-?</p></div><p style="display: none;">tjitji warta purlkana-ngka nyinarra-nyi</p><p style="display: none;">child tree big-LOC sitting-?</p><p style="clear: left;">'The child is sitting in the big tree.'</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Basque">Basque</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Basque"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque</a> has the following cases, with examples given in the indefinite, definite singular, definite plural, and definite close plural of the word <i>etxe</i>, "house", "home":
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Absolutive_case" title="Absolutive case">absolutive</a> (<i>etxe, etxe<b>a</b>, etxe<b>ak</b>, etxe<b>ok</b></i>: "house, the / a house, (the / some) houses, these houses"),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ergative_case" title="Ergative case">ergative</a> (<i>etxe<b>k</b>, etxe<b>ak</b>, etxe<b>ek</b>, etxe<b>ok</b></i>),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a> (<i>etxe<b>ri</b>, etxe<b>ari</b>, etxe<b>ei</b>, etxe<b>oi</b></i>),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> (<i>etxe<b>ren</b>, etxe<b>aren</b>, etxe<b>en</b>, etxe<b>on</b></i>),</li>
<li>destinative (or benefactive: <i>etxe<b>rentzat</b>, etxe<b>arentzat</b>, etxe<b>entzat</b>, etxe<b>ontzat</b></i>),</li>
<li>motivative (or causal: <i>etxe<b>rengatik</b>, etxe<b>arengatik</b>, etxe<b>engatik</b>, etxe<b>ongatik</b></i>),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sociative_case" title="Sociative case">sociative</a> (<i>etxe<b>rekin</b>, etxe<b>arekin</b>, etxe<b>ekin</b>, etxe<b>okin</b></i>),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Instrumental_case" title="Instrumental case">instrumental</a> (<i>etxe<b>z</b>, etxe<b>az</b>, etxe<b>ez</b>, etxe<b>oz</b></i>),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">locative</a> or inesive (<i>etxe<b>tan</b>, etxe<b>an</b>, etxe<b>etan</b>, etxe<b>otan</b></i>),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ablative_case" title="Ablative case">ablative</a> (<i>etxe<b>tatik</b>, etxe<b>tik</b>, exte<b>etatik</b>, etxe<b>otatik</b></i>),</li>
<li>adlative (<i>etxe<b>tara</b>, etxe<b>ra</b>, etxe<b>etara</b>, etxe<b>otara</b></i>),</li>
<li>directional adlative (<i>etxe<b>tarantz</b>, etxe<b>rantz</b>, etxe<b>etarantz</b>, etxe<b>otarantz</b></i>),</li>
<li>terminative adlative (<i>etxe<b>taraino</b>, etxe<b>raino</b>, etxe<b>etaraino</b>, etxe<b>otaraino</b></i>),</li>
<li>locative genitive (<i>etxe<b>tako</b>, etxe<b>ko</b>, etxe<b>etako</b>, etxe<b>otako</b></i>),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Prolative_case" title="Prolative case">prolative</a> (etxe<b>tzat</b>), only in the indefinite grammatical number,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Partitive_case" title="Partitive case">partitive</a> (etxe<b>rik</b>), only in the indefinite grammatical number, and</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Distributive_case" title="Distributive case">distributive</a> (<i>Bost liburu ikasle<b>ko</b> banatu dituzte</i>, "They have handed out five books to each student"), only in the indefinite grammatical number.</li></ul>
<p>Some of them can be re-declined, even more than once, as if they were nouns (usually, from the genitive locative case), although they mainly work as noun modifiers before a noun clause:
</p>
<ul><li><i>etxearena</i> (that which is of the house), <i>etxearenarekin</i> (with the one which pertains to the house),</li>
<li><i>neskarentzako</i> (which is for the girl), <i>neskarentzakoan</i> (in the one which is for the girl),</li>
<li><i>neskekiko</i> (which is with the girls), <i>neskekikoa</i> (the one which is for the girls),</li>
<li><i>arazoarengatiko</i> (which is because of the problem), <i>arazoarengatikoak</i> (the ones which are due to the problems),</li>
<li><i>zurezkoaz</i> (by means of the wooden one),</li>
<li><i>etxeetakoaz</i> (about the one which is in the houses), <i>etxeetakoari</i> (to the one which is in the houses),</li>
<li><i>etxetiko</i> (which comes from the house), <i>etxetikoa</i> (the one which comes from the house), etxetikoari (to the one which comes from the house),</li>
<li><i>etxeetarako</i> (which goes to the houses), <i>etxeetarakoa</i> (the one which goes to the houses), <i>etxeetarakoaz</i> (about the one which goes to the houses),</li>
<li><i>etxeranzko</i> (which goes towards the house), <i>etxeranzkoa</i> (the one which goes to the house), <i>etxeranzkoarena</i> (the one which belongs to the one which goes to the house),</li>
<li><i>etxerainoko</i> (which goes up to the house), <i>etxerainokoa</i> (the one which goes up to the house), <i>etxerainokoarekin</i> (with the one which goes up to the houses)...</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="German">German</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: German"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, grammatical case is largely preserved in the articles and adjectives, but nouns have lost many of their original endings. Below is an example of case inflection in German using the masculine <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/der#German" class="extiw" title="wikt:der">definite article</a> and one of the German words for "sailor".
</p>
<ul><li><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">d<b>er</b> Seemann</i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a>) "the sailor" [as a subject] (e.g. <i>Der Seemann steht da</i> – the sailor is standing there)</li>
<li><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">d<b>es</b> Seemann<b>(e)s</b></i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a>) "the sailor's / [of] the sailor" (e.g. <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Der Name des Seemannes ist Otto</i></span> – the name of the sailor is Otto)</li>
<li><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">d<b>em</b> Seemann<b>(e)</b></i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a>) "[to/for] the sailor" [as an indirect object] (e.g. <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Ich gab dem Seemann ein Geschenk</i></span> – I gave a present to the sailor)</li>
<li><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">d<b>en</b> Seemann</i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a>) "the sailor" [as a direct object] (e.g. <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Ich sah den Seemann</i></span> – I saw the sailor)</li></ul>
<p>An example with the feminine <a href="/wiki/Definite_article" class="mw-redirect" title="Definite article">definite article</a> with the German word for "woman".
</p>
<ul><li><i>d<b>ie</b> Frau</i> (<a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a>) "the woman" [as a subject] (e.g. <i>Die Frau isst</i> - the woman eats)</li>
<li><i>d<b>er</b> Frau</i> (<a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a>) "the woman's / [of] the woman" (e.g. <i>Die Katze der Frau ist weiß</i> - the cat of the woman is white)</li>
<li><i>d<b>er</b> Frau</i> (<a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a>) "[to/for] the woman" [as an indirect object] (e.g. <i>Ich gab der Frau ein Geschenk</i> - I gave a present to the woman)</li>
<li><i>d<b>ie</b> Frau</i> (<a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a>) "the woman" [as a direct object] (e.g. <i>Ich sah die Frau</i> - I saw the woman)</li></ul>
<p>An example with the neuter <a href="/wiki/Definite_article" class="mw-redirect" title="Definite article">definite article</a> with the German word for "book".
</p>
<ul><li><i>d<b>as</b> Buch (<a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a>)</i> "the book" [as a subject] (e.g. <i>Das Buch ist gut -</i> the book is good)</li>
<li><i>d<b>es</b> Buch<b>(e)s</b></i> (<a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a>) "the book's/ [of] the book" (e.g. <i>Die Seiten des Buchs sind grün</i> - the pages of the book are green)</li>
<li><i>d<b>em</b> Buch<b>(e)</b></i> (<a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a>) "[to/for] the book" [as an indirect object] (e.g. <i>Ich gab dem Buch einen Titel</i> - I gave the book a title)</li>
<li><i>d<b>as</b> Buch</i> (<a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a>) "the book" [as a direct object] (e.g. <i>Ich sah das Buch</i> - I saw the book)</li></ul>
<p>Proper names for cities have two <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> nouns:
</p>
<ul><li><i>der Hauptbahnhof Berlin<b>s</b></i> (primary <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a>) "the main train station of Berlin"</li>
<li><i>der Berlin<b>er</b> Hauptbahnhof</i> (secondary <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a>) "Berlin's main train station"</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Hindi-Urdu">Hindi-Urdu</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Hindi-Urdu"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindi</a>-<a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hindustani_grammar" title="Hindustani grammar">Hindustani</a>) has three noun cases, the <i>nominative,</i> <i>oblique</i>, and <i>vocative</i> cases. The vocative case is now obsolete (but still used in certain regions<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>) and the oblique case doubles as the vocative case. The pronoun cases in Hindi-Urdu are the <i>nominative</i>, <i>ergative</i>, <i>accusative, dative</i>, and two <i>oblique</i> cases.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The case forms which do not exist for certain pronouns are constructed using primary postpositions (or other <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_particle" title="Grammatical particle">grammatical particles</a>) and the oblique case (shown in parentheses in the table below).
</p><p>The other cases are constructed <a href="/wiki/Preposition_and_postposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Preposition and postposition">adpositionally</a> using the case-marking postpositions using the nouns and pronouns in their oblique cases. The oblique case is used exclusively with these 8 case-marking postpositions of Hindi-Urdu forming 10 grammatical cases, which are: <i>ergative</i> ने (ne), <i>dative</i> and <i>accusative</i> को (ko), <i>instrumental</i> and <i>ablative</i> से (se), <i>genitive</i> का (kā), <i>inessive</i> में (mẽ), <i>adessive</i> पे (pe), <i>terminative</i> तक (tak), <i>semblative</i> सा (sā).<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Latin">Latin</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Latin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An example of a <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> case inflection is given below, using the singular forms of the Latin term for "cook", which belongs to <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Latin_second_declension" class="extiw" title="wikt:Appendix:Latin second declension">Latin's second declension class</a>.
</p>
<ul><li><i lang="la"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/coquus#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:coquus">coqu<b>us</b></a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a>) "[the] cook" [as a subject] (e.g. <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">coquus ibī stat</i></span> – the cook is standing there)</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">coqu<b>ī</b></i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a>) "[the] cook's / [of the] cook" (e.g. <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">nōmen coquī Claudius est</i></span> – the cook's name is Claudius)</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">coqu<b>ō</b></i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a>) "[to/for the] cook" [as an indirect object] (e.g. <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">coquō dōnum dedī</i></span> – I gave a present to the cook)</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">coqu<b>um</b></i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a>) "[the] cook" [as a direct object] (e.g. <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">coquum vīdī</i></span> – I saw the cook)</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">coqu<b>ō</b></i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Ablative_case" title="Ablative case">ablative</a>) "[by/with/from/in the] cook" [in various uses not covered by the above] (e.g. <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">sum altior coquō</i></span> – I am taller than the cook: ablative of comparison)</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">coqu<b>e</b></i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Vocative_case" title="Vocative case">vocative</a>) "[you] the cook" [addressing the object] (e.g. <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">grātiās tibi agō, coque</i></span> – I thank you, cook)</li></ul>
<p>For some toponyms, a seventh case, the <a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">locative</a>, also exists, such as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Mediolān<b>ī</b></i></span> (in <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Mediolanum</a>).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a> have largely abandoned or simplified the grammatical cases of Latin. Much like English, most Romance case markers survive only in pronouns.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Lithuanian">Lithuanian</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Lithuanian"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Typically in <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a>, only the inflection changes for the seven different grammatical cases:
</p>
<ul><li>Nominative (<i><i lang="lt"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vardininkas#Lithuanian" class="extiw" title="wikt:vardininkas">vardininkas</a></i></i>): <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">šuo</i></span> – <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">Tai yra šuo</i></span> – "This is a dog."</li>
<li>Genitive (<i><i lang="lt"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/kilmininkas#Lithuanian" class="extiw" title="wikt:kilmininkas">kilmininkas</a></i></i>): <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">šuns</i></span> – <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">Tomas paėmė šuns kaulą</i></span> – "Tom took the dog's bone."</li>
<li>Dative (<i><i lang="lt"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/naudininkas#Lithuanian" class="extiw" title="wikt:naudininkas">naudininkas</a></i></i>): <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">šuniui</i></span> – <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">Jis davė kaulą kitam šuniui</i></span> – "He gave the bone to another dog."</li>
<li>Accusative (<i><i lang="lt"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/galininkas#Lithuanian" class="extiw" title="wikt:galininkas">galininkas</a></i></i>): <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">šunį</i></span> – <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">Jis nuprausė šunį</i></span> – "He washed the dog."</li>
<li>Instrumental (<i><i lang="lt"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C4%AFnagininkas#Lithuanian" class="extiw" title="wikt:įnagininkas">įnagininkas</a></i></i>): <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">šunimi</i></span> – <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">Jis šunimi išgąsdino kates</i></span> – He scared the cats with (using) the dog.</li>
<li>Locative (<i><i lang="lt"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vietininkas#Lithuanian" class="extiw" title="wikt:vietininkas">vietininkas</a></i></i>): <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">šunyje</i></span> – <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">Susitiksime „Baltame šunyje“</i></span> – "We'll meet at the White Dog (Cafe)."</li>
<li>Vocative (<i><i lang="lt"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C5%A1auksmininkas#Lithuanian" class="extiw" title="wikt:šauksmininkas">šauksmininkas</a></i></i>): <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">šunie</i></span> – <span title="Lithuanian-language text"><i lang="lt">Jis sušuko: Ei, šunie!</i></span> – "He shouted: Hey, dog!"</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Hungarian">Hungarian</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hungarian"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a> declension is relatively simple with regular suffixes attached to the vast majority of nouns. The following table lists all of the cases used in Hungarian.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption><b><span lang="hu"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/h%C3%A1z#Hungarian" class="extiw" title="wikt:ház">ház</a></span></b> – house, <span lang="hu"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/kett%C5%91#Hungarian" class="extiw" title="wikt:kettő">kettő</a></span> – two
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Case
</th>
<th>Meaning
</th>
<th>Suffix
</th>
<th>Example
</th>
<th>Meaning of the example
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">Nominative case</a>
</td>
<td>subject
</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">∅</span>
</td>
<td><span title="Hungarian-language text"><i lang="hu">ház</i></span>
</td>
<td>house (as a subject)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">Accusative case</a>
</td>
<td>direct object
</td>
<td><span title="Hungarian-language text"><i lang="hu">-ot/(-at)/-et/-öt/-t</i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Hungarian-language text"><i lang="hu">házat</i></span>
</td>
<td>house (as an object)
</td></tr>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Russian">Russian</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Russian"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Russian_declension" title="Russian declension">Russian declension</a></div>
<p>An example of a <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> case inflection is given below (with explicit stress marks), using the singular forms of the Russian term for "sailor", which belongs to Russian's first declension class.
</p>
<ul><li><span lang="ru"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%8F%D0%BA#Russian" class="extiw" title="wikt:моряк">моря́к</a></span> (<a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a>) "[the] sailor" [as a subject] (e.g. <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Там стоит моряк</span></span>: The sailor is standing there)</li>
<li><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">моряк<b>á</b></span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a>) "[the] sailor's / [of the] sailor" (e.g. <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Сын моряка — художник</span></span>: The sailor's son is an artist)</li>
<li><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">моряк<b>у́</b></span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a>) "[to/for the] sailor" [as an indirect object] (e.g. <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Моряку подарили подарок</span></span>: (They/Someone) gave a present to the sailor)</li>
<li><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">моряк<b>á</b></span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a>) "[the] sailor" [as a direct object] (e.g. <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Вижу моряка</span></span>: (I) see the sailor)</li>
<li><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">моряк<b>о́м</b></span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Instrumental_case" title="Instrumental case">instrumental</a>) "[with/by the] sailor" (e.g. <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Дружу с моряком</span></span>: (I) have a friendship with the sailor)</li>
<li><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">о/на/в моряк<b>е́</b></span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Prepositional_case" class="mw-redirect" title="Prepositional case">prepositional</a>) "[about/on/in the] sailor" (e.g. <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Думаю о моряке</span></span>: (I) think about the sailor)</li></ul>
<p>Up to ten additional cases are identified by linguists, although today all of them are either incomplete (do not apply to all nouns or do not form full word paradigm with all combinations of gender and number) or degenerate (appear identical to one of the main six cases). The most recognized additional cases are locative (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">в лесу́, на мосту́, в слеза́х</span></span>), partitive (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">ча́ю, са́хару, песку́</span></span>), and two forms of vocative — old (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Го́споди, Бо́же, о́тче</span></span>) and neo-vocative (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Маш, пап, ребя́т</span></span>). Sometimes, so called count-form (for some countable nouns after numerals) is considered to be a sub-case.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Sanskrit"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Grammatical case was analyzed extensively in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>. The grammarian <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a> identified six semantic <a href="/wiki/Theta_role" title="Theta role">roles</a> or <i>kāraka</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which are related to the following eight Sanskrit cases in order:<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<table>
