<p>For example, in the following sentence <i>leaf</i> is the agent (<i>kartā</i>, nominative case), <i>tree</i> is the source (<i>apādāna</i>, ablative case), and <i>ground</i> is the locus (<i>adhikaraṇa</i>, locative case). The <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_nouns#Basic_noun_and_adjective_declension&quot;" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanskrit nouns">declensions</a> are reflected in the morphemes <i>-āt</i>, <i>-am</i>, and <i>-au</i> respectively.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1209690778"><div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">vṛkṣ-āt</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><b>from</b> the tree</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">parṇ-am</p><p style="margin: 0px;">a leaf</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">bhūm-au</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><b>on</b> the ground</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">patati</p><p style="margin: 0px;">falls</p></div><p style="display: none;">vṛkṣ-āt parṇ-am bhūm-au patati</p><p style="display: none;">{<b>from</b> the tree} {a leaf} {<b>on</b> the ground} falls</p><p class="mw-empty-elt"></p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<p>However, the cases may be deployed for other than the default thematic roles. A notable example is the passive construction. In the following sentence, <i>Devadatta</i> is the <i>kartā</i>, but appears in the instrumental case, and <i>rice</i>, the <i>karman</i>, object, is in the nominative case (as subject of the verb). The <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_nouns#Basic_noun_and_adjective_declension&quot;" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanskrit nouns">declensions</a> are reflected in the morphemes <i>-ena</i> and <i>-am</i>.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1209690778"><div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">devadatt-ena</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><b>by</b> Devadatta</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">odan-am</p><p style="margin: 0px;">the rice</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">pacyate</p><p style="margin: 0px;">is cooked</p></div><p style="display: none;">devadatt-ena odan-am pacyate</p><p style="display: none;">{<b>by</b> Devadatta} {the rice} {is cooked}</p><p class="mw-empty-elt"></p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Tamil">Tamil</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Tamil"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a> case system is analyzed in native and missionary grammars as consisting of a finite number of cases.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JSTOR600654_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JSTOR600654-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The usual treatment of Tamil case (Arden 1942)<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is one in which there are seven cases: nominative (first case), accusative (second case), instrumental (third), dative (fourth), ablative (fifth), genitive (sixth), and locative (seventh). In traditional analyses, there is always a clear distinction made between post-positional morphemes and case endings. The vocative is sometimes given a place in the case system as an eighth case, but vocative forms do not participate in usual morphophonemic alternations and do not govern the use of any postpositions. Modern grammarians, however, argue that this eight-case classification is coarse and artificial<sup id="cite_ref-JSTOR600654_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JSTOR600654-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and that Tamil usage is best understood if each suffix or combination of suffixes is seen as marking a separate case.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="2">Case</th>
<th colspan="2">Suffixes</th>
<th>Example: மன்னன் (mannan) [king]
</th></tr>

<li>மன்னனில் (mannanil)</li>
<li>மன்னனிலிருந்து (mannanilirundu)</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Sixth case</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">Genitive</a></td>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Turkish">Turkish</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Turkish"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Modern Turkish has six cases (In Turkish <i>İsmin Hâlleri</i>).
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>
</th>
<th>Nominative<br />What? Who?
</th>
<th>Accusative<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>b<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><br /> What? Who?
</th>
<th>Dative<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>c<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>d<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>e<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><br /> To whom?
</th>
<th>Locative<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>f<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>g<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><br /> Where? Whom?
</th>
<th>Ablative<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>h<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>i<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><br /> Where from? From whom? Why?
</th>
<th>Genitive<br /> Whose? What's wrong?
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th>Singular
</th>
<td>çiçe<b>k</b> / (a/the) flower (nom)
</td>
<td>çiçe<b>ği</b> / (a/the) flower (acc)
</td>
<td>çiçe<b>ğe</b> / to (a/the) flower
</td>
<td>çiçek<b>te</b> / in (a/the) flower
</td>
<td>çiçe<b>kten</b> / from (a/the) flower
</td>
<td>çiçe<b>ğin</b> / of (a/the) flower
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Plural
</th>
<td>çiçe<b>kler</b> / (the) flowers (nom)
</td>
<td>çiçe<b>kleri</b> / (the) flowers (acc)
</td>
<td>çiçe<b>klere</b> / to (the) flowers
</td>
<td>çiçe<b>klerde</b> / in (the) flowers
</td>
<td>çiçe<b>klerden</b> / from (the) flowers
</td>
<td>çiçe<b>klerin</b> / of (the) flowers
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The accusative can exist only in the noun(whether it is derived from a verb or not). For example, "Arkadaşlar bize gel<b>meyi</b> düşünüyorlar." (Friends are thinking of com<b>ing</b> to us).
</p><p>The dative can exist only in the noun (whether it is derived from a verb or not). For example, "Bol bol kitap oku<b>maya</b> çalışıyorum." (I try <b>to read</b> a lot of books).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Evolution">Evolution</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Evolution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>As languages evolve, case systems change. In early Ancient Greek, for example, the genitive and ablative cases of given names became combined, giving five cases, rather than the six retained in Latin. In modern <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>, the cases have been reduced to three: a direct case (for subjects and direct objects) and <a href="/wiki/Oblique_case" title="Oblique case">oblique case</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Vocative_case" title="Vocative case">vocative case</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In English, apart from the pronouns discussed above, case has vanished altogether except for the possessive/non-possessive dichotomy in nouns.
</p><p>The evolution of the treatment of case relationships can be circular.<sup id="cite_ref-Blake_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blake-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: pp.167–174">&#58;&#8202;pp.167–174&#8202;</span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Postposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Postposition">Postpositions</a> can become unstressed and sound like they are an unstressed syllable of a neighboring word. A postposition can thus merge into the stem of a head noun, developing various forms depending on the phonological shape of the stem. Affixes are subject to various phonological processes such as <a href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">assimilation</a>, vowel centering to the <a href="/wiki/Schwa" class="mw-redirect" title="Schwa">schwa</a>, phoneme loss, and <a href="/wiki/Fusion_(phonetics)" title="Fusion (phonetics)">fusion</a>, and these processes can reduce or even eliminate the distinctions between cases. Languages can then compensate for the resulting loss of function by creating postpositions, thus coming full circle.
</p><p>Recent experiments in agent-based modeling have shown how case systems can emerge and evolve in a population of language users.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The experiments demonstrate that language users may introduce new case markers to reduce the cognitive effort required for semantic interpretation, hence facilitating communication through language. Case markers then become generalized through analogical reasoning and reuse.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Linguistic_typology">Linguistic typology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Linguistic typology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Morphosyntactic_alignment">Morphosyntactic alignment</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Morphosyntactic alignment"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Morphosyntactic_alignment" title="Morphosyntactic alignment">Morphosyntactic alignment</a></div>
<p>Languages are categorized into several case systems, based on their <i>morphosyntactic alignment</i>—how they group verb <a href="/wiki/Agent_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Agent (linguistics)">agents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patient_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Patient (linguistics)">patients</a> into cases:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Nominative%E2%80%93accusative_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominative–accusative language">Nominative–accusative</a></i> (or simply <i>accusative</i>): The argument (subject) of an intransitive verb is in the same case as the agent (subject) of a transitive verb; this case is then called the <i><a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative case</a></i>, with the patient (direct object) of a transitive verb being in the <i><a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative case</a></i>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Ergative%E2%80%93absolutive_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ergative–absolutive language">Ergative–absolutive</a></i> (or simply <i>ergative</i>): The argument (subject) of an intransitive verb is in the same case as the patient (direct object) of a transitive verb; this case is then called the <i><a href="/wiki/Absolutive_case" title="Absolutive case">absolutive case</a></i>, with the agent (subject) of a transitive verb being in the <i><a href="/wiki/Ergative_case" title="Ergative case">ergative case</a></i>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Ergative%E2%80%93accusative_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ergative–accusative language">Ergative–accusative</a></i> (or <i>tripartite</i>): The argument (subject) of an intransitive verb is in its own case (the <i><a href="/wiki/Intransitive_case" title="Intransitive case">intransitive case</a></i>), separate from that of the agent (subject) or patient (direct object) of a transitive verb (which is in the ergative case or accusative case, respectively).</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Active%E2%80%93stative_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Active–stative language">Active–stative</a></i> (or simply <i>active</i>): The argument (subject) of an intransitive verb can be in one of two cases; if the argument is an <i>agent</i>, as in "He ate", then it is in the same case as the agent (subject) of a transitive verb (sometimes called the <i>agentive case</i>), and if it is a <i>patient</i>, as in "He tripped", then it is in the same case as the patient (direct object) of a transitive verb (sometimes called the <i>patientive case</i>).</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Trigger_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Trigger language">Trigger</a></i>: One noun in a sentence is the topic or focus. This noun is in the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trigger_case&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Trigger case (page does not exist)">trigger case</a>, and information elsewhere in the sentence (for example a <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">verb</a> <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affix</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">Tagalog</a>) specifies the role of the trigger. The trigger may be identified as the agent, patient, etc. Other nouns may be inflected for case, but the inflections are overloaded; for example, in Tagalog, the subject and object of a verb are both expressed in the <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive case</a> when they are not in the trigger case.</li></ul>
<p>The following are systems that some languages use to mark case instead of, or in addition to, declension:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Positional</b>: Nouns are not inflected for case; the position of a noun in the sentence expresses its case.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adposition" title="Adposition">Adpositional</a>: Nouns are accompanied by words that mark case.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Language_families">Language families</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grammatical_case&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Language families"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<ul><li>With a few exceptions, most languages in the <a href="/wiki/Finno-Ugric_languages" title="Finno-Ugric languages">Finno-Ugric</a> family make extensive use of cases. <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a> has 15 cases according to the traditional description (or up to 30 depending on the interpretation).<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, only 12 are commonly used in speech (see <a href="/wiki/Finnish_noun_cases" title="Finnish noun cases">Finnish noun cases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finnish_locative_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Finnish locative system">Finnish locative system</a>). <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a> has 14 (see <a href="/wiki/Estonian_locative_system" title="Estonian locative system">Estonian locative system</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a> has 18, both with additional archaic cases used for some words.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongolic_languages" title="Mongolic languages">Mongolic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tungusic_languages" title="Tungusic languages">Tungusic</a> languages also exhibit complex case systems. Since the abovementioned languages, along with <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>, shared certain similarities, linguists proposed an <a href="/wiki/Altaic_languages" title="Altaic languages">Altaic</a> family and reconstructed its case system; although the hypothesis had been largely discredited.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Tsez_language" title="Tsez language">Tsez language</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Caucasian_languages" title="Northeast Caucasian languages">Northeast Caucasian language</a>, has 64 cases.</li>
<li>The original version of John Quijada's <a href="/wiki/Constructed_language" title="Constructed language">constructed language</a> <a href="/wiki/Ithkuil" title="Ithkuil">Ithkuil</a> has 81 noun cases,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and its descendant <a href="/wiki/Ilaksh" class="mw-redirect" title="Ilaksh">Ilaksh</a> and Ithkuil after the 2011 revision both have 96 noun cases.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lemma_(morphology)" title="Lemma (morphology)">lemma</a> form of words, which is the form chosen by convention as the canonical form of a word, is usually the most <a href="/wiki/Markedness" title="Markedness">unmarked</a> or basic case, which is typically the nominative, trigger, or absolutive case, whichever a language may have.
</p>

<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/English_possessive#Status_of_the_possessive_as_a_grammatical_case" title="English possessive">status of the possessive</a> as an affix or a clitic is the subject of debate.<sup id="cite_ref-Hudson_2013_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hudson_2013-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Börjars_2013_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Börjars_2013-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It differs from the noun inflection of languages such as German, in that the genitive ending may attach to the last word of the phrase. To account for this, the possessive can be analysed, for instance as a clitic construction (an "enclitic postposition"<sup id="cite_ref-Quirk_group_genitive_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quirk_group_genitive-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>) or as an inflection<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford_English_Grammar,_Case_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_English_Grammar,_Case-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> of the last word of a phrase ("edge inflection").<sup id="cite_ref-Huddleston_phrasal_genitive_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huddleston_phrasal_genitive-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Yaşamı sevmek, gazeteyi okumak, camları silmek, ödevini yapmak, sesini duymak, kapıyı açmak, üzümü toplamak. <b>Not</b>: Saat yedi<b>yi</b> beş geçiyor. Üç<b>ü</b> çeyrek geçiyor.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Saat dokuz<b>a</b> on var. On iki<b>ye</b> çeyrek var. Kaç liraya? Kaça?</span>

<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Edatlardan –e ile bağlananlar: bize göre, bize karşı, her şeye karşın, kışa doğru, o konuya dair, size ait, yağmura karşın, iyiliklerine karşılık</span>

<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>ben, sen</i>person pronouns: Ben-e&gt; bana, sen-e&gt;sana</span>

<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"L. <i lang="la"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/casus#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:casus">cāsus</a></i> used to translate Gr. <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">πτῶσις</span></span> lit. 'falling, fall'. By <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">πτῶσις</span></span> was applied to any derived, inflected, or extended form of the simple <span lang="grc"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BD%84%CE%BD%CE%BF%CE%BC%CE%B1#Ancient_Greek" class="extiw" title="wikt:ὄνομα">ὄνομα</a></span> or <span lang="grc"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BF%A5%E1%BF%86%CE%BC%CE%B1#Ancient_Greek" class="extiw" title="wikt:ῥῆμα">ῥῆμα</a></span> (i.e. the nominative of nouns, the present indicative of verbs), such as the oblique cases of nouns, the variations of adjectives due to gender and comparison, also the derived adverb (e.g. <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">δικαίως</span></span> was a <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">πτῶσις</span></span> of <span lang="grc"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B4%CE%AF%CE%BA%CE%B1%CE%B9%CE%BF%CF%82#Ancient_Greek" class="extiw" title="wikt:δίκαιος">δίκαιος</a></span>), the other tenses and moods of the verb, including its interrogative form. The grammarians, following the <a href="/wiki/Stoics" class="mw-redirect" title="Stoics">Stoics</a>, restricted <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">πτῶσις</span></span> to nouns, and included the nominative under the designation". <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><cite id="CITEREFReference-OED-case" class="citation encyclopaedia cs1"><span class="id-lock-subscription" title="Paid subscription required"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.oed.com/search/dictionary/?q=case">"case"</a></span>. <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> (Online&#160;ed.). <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">Oxford University Press</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=case&amp;rft.btitle=Oxford+English+Dictionary&amp;rft.edition=Online&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.oed.com%2Fsearch%2Fdictionary%2F%3Fq%3Dcase&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGrammatical+case" class="Z3988"></span>&#32;<span style="font-size:0.95em; font-size:95%; color: var( --color-subtle, #555 )">(Subscription or <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.oed.com/public/login/loggingin#withyourlibrary">participating institution membership</a> required.)</span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Oxford_English_Grammar,_Case-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Oxford_English_Grammar,_Case_14-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><cite id="CITEREFGreenbaum1996" class="citation book cs1"><a href="/wiki/Sidney_Greenbaum" title="Sidney Greenbaum">Greenbaum, Sidney</a> (1996). <i>The Oxford English Grammar</i>. <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">Oxford University Press</a>. pp.&#160;109–110. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-861250-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-861250-6"><bdi>978-0-19-861250-6</bdi></a>. <q>In speech the genitive is signalled in singular nouns by an inflection that has the same pronunciation variants as for plural nouns in the common case</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Oxford+English+Grammar&amp;rft.pages=109-110&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1996&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-861250-6&amp;rft.aulast=Greenbaum&amp;rft.aufirst=Sidney&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGrammatical+case" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><cite id="CITEREFQuirkGreenbaumLeechSvartik1985" class="citation book cs1">Quirk, Randolph; Greenbaum, Sidney; Leech, Geoffrey; Svartik, Jan (1985). <span class="id-lock-registration" title="Free registration required"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/comprehensivegra00quir"><i>A Comprehensive Grammar of the English Language</i></a></span>. Longman. p.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/comprehensivegra00quir/page/319">319</a>. <q>In writing, the inflection of regular nouns is realized in the singular by apostrophe + <i>s</i> (<i>boy's</i>), and in the regular plural by the apostrophe following the plural <i>s</i> (<i>boys<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span></i>)</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+Comprehensive+Grammar+of+the+English+Language&amp;rft.pages=319&amp;rft.pub=Longman&amp;rft.date=1985&amp;rft.aulast=Quirk&amp;rft.aufirst=Randolph&amp;rft.au=Greenbaum%2C+Sidney&amp;rft.au=Leech%2C+Geoffrey&amp;rft.au=Svartik%2C+Jan&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fcomprehensivegra00quir&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGrammatical+case" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Huddleston_phrasal_genitive-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Huddleston_phrasal_genitive_16-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><cite id="CITEREFPayneHuddleston2002" class="citation book cs1">Payne, John; <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Huddleston" title="Rodney Huddleston">Huddleston, Rodney</a> (2002). "Nouns and noun phrases". In <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Huddleston" title="Rodney Huddleston">Huddleston, Rodney</a>; <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Pullum" class="mw-redirect" title="Geoffrey Pullum">Pullum, Geoffrey</a> (eds.). <i>The Cambridge Grammar of the English Language</i>. Cambridge; New York: Cambridge University Press. pp.&#160;479–481. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-43146-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-521-43146-0"><bdi>978-0-521-43146-0</bdi></a>. <q>We conclude that both head and phrasal genitives involve case inflection. With head genitives it is always a noun that inflects, while the phrasal genitive can apply to words of most classes.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Nouns+and+noun+phrases&amp;rft.btitle=The+Cambridge+Grammar+of+the+English+Language&amp;rft.place=Cambridge%3B+New+York&amp;rft.pages=479-481&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-521-43146-0&amp;rft.aulast=Payne&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rft.au=Huddleston%2C+Rodney&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGrammatical+case" class="Z3988"></span></span>
